      This refers to the historic county in England. For other uses, either see
      <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sussex_(disambiguation)" title="Sussex (disambiguation)">
       Sussex (disambiguation)
      </a>
      or, for the administrative counties that Sussex is divided into,
      <a href="/wiki/East_Sussex" title="East Sussex">
       East Sussex
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/West_Sussex" title="West Sussex">
       West Sussex
      </a>
      .

     <p>
      <b>
       Sussex
      </b>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">
           ˈ
          </span>
          <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
           s
          </span>
          <span title="/ʌ/ short 'u' in 'bud'">
           ʌ
          </span>
          <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
           s
          </span>
          <span title="/ɪ/ or /ə/ 'e' in 'roses'">
           ᵻ
          </span>
          <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
           k
          </span>
          <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
           s
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      ; abbreviated
      <b>
       Sx
      </b>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langscape_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Langscape-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      from the
      <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
       Old English
      </a>
      <i>
       Sūþsēaxe
      </i>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Saxons" title="South Saxons">
       South Saxons
      </a>
      ), is a
      <a href="/wiki/Historic_counties_of_England" title="Historic counties of England">
       historic county
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/South_East_England" title="South East England">
       South East England
      </a>
      corresponding roughly in area to the
      <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">
       ancient
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sussex" title="Kingdom of Sussex">
       Kingdom of Sussex
      </a>
      . It is bounded to the west by
      <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">
       Hampshire
      </a>
      , north by
      <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">
       Surrey
      </a>
      , north-east by
      <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">
       Kent
      </a>
      , south by the
      <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">
       English Channel
      </a>
      , and divided for local government into
      <a href="/wiki/West_Sussex" title="West Sussex">
       West Sussex
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/East_Sussex" title="East Sussex">
       East Sussex
      </a>
      and the city of
      <a href="/wiki/Brighton_and_Hove" title="Brighton and Hove">
       Brighton and Hove
      </a>
      . Brighton and Hove was created as a
      <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authority" title="Unitary authority">
       unitary authority
      </a>
      in 1997, and granted
      <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">
       City status
      </a>
      in 2000. Until then,
      <a href="/wiki/Chichester" title="Chichester">
       Chichester
      </a>
      was Sussex's only city.
     </p>
     <p>
      Sussex has three main geographic sub-regions, each oriented approximately east to west. In the south-west is the fertile and densely populated
      <a href="/wiki/Coastal_plain" title="Coastal plain">
       coastal plain
      </a>
      . North of this are the rolling
      <a href="/wiki/Chalk" title="Chalk">
       chalk
      </a>
      hills of the
      <a href="/wiki/South_Downs" title="South Downs">
       South Downs
      </a>
      , beyond which is the well-wooded
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sussex_Weald" title="Sussex Weald">
       Sussex Weald
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The name derives from the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sussex" title="Kingdom of Sussex">
       Kingdom of Sussex
      </a>
      , which was founded, according to legend, by
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lle_of_Sussex" title="Ælle of Sussex">
       Ælle of Sussex
      </a>
      in AD 477. In 825, it was absorbed into the kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">
       Wessex
      </a>
      and subsequently into the kingdom of England. It was the home of some of Europe's earliest
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">
       hominids
      </a>
      , whose remains have been found at at
      <a href="/wiki/Boxgrove" title="Boxgrove">
       Boxgrove
      </a>
      , and was invaded by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_invasion_of_Britain" title="Roman invasion of Britain">
       Romans
      </a>
      and is the site of the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">
       Battle of Hastings
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1974, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord-Lieutenant" title="Lord-Lieutenant">
       Lord-Lieutenant
      </a>
      of Sussex was replaced with one each for East and West Sussex, which became separate ceremonial counties. Sussex continues to be recognised as a geographical territory and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_region" title="Cultural region">
       cultural region
      </a>
      . It has had a single police force since 1968 and its name is in common use in the media.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCSx_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBCSx-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2007,
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_Day" title="Sussex Day">
       Sussex Day
      </a>
      was created to celebrate the county's rich
      <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Sussex" title="Culture of Sussex">
       culture
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sussex" title="History of Sussex">
       history
      </a>
      . Based on the traditional emblem of Sussex, a blue shield with six gold
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martlets" title="Martlets">
       martlets
      </a>
      , the flag of Sussex was recognised by the Flag Institute in 2011. In 2013,
      <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Communities_and_Local_Government" title="Secretary of State for Communities and Local Government">
       Secretary of State for Communities and Local Government
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Eric_Pickles" title="Eric Pickles">
       Eric Pickles
      </a>
      formally recognised and acknowledged the continued existence of England's 39 historic counties, including Sussex.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CLG-EP_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CLG-EP-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind-EP_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ind-EP-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC-EP_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ABC-EP-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">
       Toponymy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Toponymy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The name "Sussex" is derived from the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
       Middle English
      </a>
      <i>
       Suth-sæxe
      </i>
      , which is in turn derived from the
      <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
       Old English
      </a>
      <i>
       Suth-Seaxe
      </i>
      which means (land or people) of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Saxons" title="South Saxons">
       South Saxons
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WebNWCD_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WebNWCD-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (cf.
      <a href="/wiki/Essex#History" title="Essex">
       Essex
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Middlesex#Toponomy" title="Middlesex">
       Middlesex
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">
       Wessex
      </a>
      ). The South Saxons were a
      <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">
       Germanic tribe
      </a>
      that settled in the region from the
      <a href="/wiki/North_German_Plain" title="North German Plain">
       North German Plain
      </a>
      during the 5th and 6th centuries.
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest known usage of the term South Saxons (Latin:
      <i>
       Australes Saxones
      </i>
      ) is in a royal charter of 689 which names them and their king,
      <a href="/wiki/Nothhelm_of_Sussex" title="Nothhelm of Sussex">
       Noðhelm
      </a>
      , although the term may well have been in use for some time before that. The monastic chronicler who wrote up the entry classifying the invasion seems to have got his dates wrong; recent scholars have suggested he might have been a quarter of a century too late.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowerson_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lowerson-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">
       New Latin
      </a>
      word
      <i>
       Suthsexia
      </i>
      was used for Sussex by Dutch cartographer
      <a href="/wiki/Joan_Blaeu" title="Joan Blaeu">
       Joan Blaeu
      </a>
      in his 1645 map.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NLA_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NLA-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Three
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/County_(United_States)" title="County (United States)">
       counties
      </a>
      (in
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_County,_Delaware" title="Sussex County, Delaware">
       Delaware
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_County,_New_Jersey" title="Sussex County, New Jersey">
       New Jersey
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_County,_Virginia" title="Sussex County, Virginia">
       Virginia
      </a>
      ), and a former
      <a href="/wiki/Lands_administrative_divisions_of_Western_Australia" title="Lands administrative divisions of Western Australia">
       county/land division
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_County,_Western_Australia" title="Sussex County, Western Australia">
       Western Australia
      </a>
      , are named after Sussex.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">
       Symbols
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Symbols">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Symbols_of_Sussex" title="Symbols of Sussex">
       Symbols of Sussex
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sussex_shield.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="240" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sussex_shield.svg/160px-Sussex_shield.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sussex_shield.svg/240px-Sussex_shield.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sussex_shield.svg/320px-Sussex_shield.svg.png 2x" width="160"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sussex_shield.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The traditional emblem of Sussex first produced in 1622 by John Speed:
        <i>
         Azure, six
         <a href="/wiki/Martlet" title="Martlet">
          martlets
         </a>
         or
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Sussex" title="Flag of Sussex">
       flag of Sussex
      </a>
      consists of six gold
      <a href="/wiki/Martlet" title="Martlet">
       martlets
      </a>
      , or heraldic
      <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">
       swallows
      </a>
      , on a blue background,
      <a href="/wiki/Blazon" title="Blazon">
       blazoned
      </a>
      as
      <i>
       Azure, six
       <a href="/wiki/Martlet" title="Martlet">
        martlets
       </a>
       or
      </i>
      . Officially recognised by the
      <a href="/wiki/Flag_Institute" title="Flag Institute">
       Flag Institute
      </a>
      on 20 May 2011, its design is based on the
      <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Sussex" title="Coat of arms of Sussex">
       heraldic shield of Sussex
      </a>
      that first appeared in an atlas by
      <a href="/wiki/John_Speed" title="John Speed">
       John Speed
      </a>
      in 1622. The significance of the six martlets may be to represent the traditional six sub-divisions of the county known as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rape_(county_subdivision)" title="Rape (county subdivision)">
        rapes
       </a>
      </i>
      . It may also be a
      <a href="/wiki/Canting_arms" title="Canting arms">
       canting
      </a>
      reference to the title of the historic leading Sussex family, the
      <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Arundel" title="Earl of Arundel">
       Earls of Arundel
      </a>
      , seated at
      <a href="/wiki/Arundel_Castle" title="Arundel Castle">
       Arundel Castle
      </a>
      in the county, as the French for swallow is
      <i>
       hirondelle
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="769" data-file-width="1023" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG/200px-Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG/300px-Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG/400px-Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Campanulaceae_-_Phyteuma_orbiculare.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Phyteuma_orbiculare" title="Phyteuma orbiculare">
         round-headed rampion
        </a>
        , or Pride of Sussex is Sussex's county flower
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sussex_by_the_Sea" title="Sussex by the Sea">
        Sussex by the Sea
       </a>
      </i>
      is regarded as the unofficial anthem of Sussex; it was composed by
      <a href="/wiki/William_Ward-Higgs" title="William Ward-Higgs">
       William Ward-Higgs
      </a>
      in 1907, perhaps originally from the lyrics of
      <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">
       Rudyard Kipling
      </a>
      's poem entitled
      <i>
       Sussex
      </i>
      . Adopted by the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Sussex_Regiment" title="Royal Sussex Regiment">
       Royal Sussex Regiment
      </a>
      and popularised in
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      , it is sung at celebrations across the county, including those at
      <a href="/wiki/Lewes_Bonfire" title="Lewes Bonfire">
       Lewes Bonfire
      </a>
      , and at sports matches, including those of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brighton_and_Hove_Albion_Football_Club" title="Brighton and Hove Albion Football Club">
       Brighton and Hove Albion Football Club
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_County_Cricket_Club" title="Sussex County Cricket Club">
       Sussex County Cricket Club
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The county day, called
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_Day" title="Sussex Day">
       Sussex Day
      </a>
      , is celebrated on 16 June, the same day as the feast day of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Richard_of_Chichester" title="St Richard of Chichester">
       St Richard of Chichester
      </a>
      , Sussex's
      <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">
       patron saint
      </a>
      , whose shrine at
      <a href="/wiki/Chichester_Cathedral" title="Chichester Cathedral">
       Chichester Cathedral
      </a>
      was an important place of pilgrimage in the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
       Middle Ages
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Sussex's motto,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/We_wunt_be_druv" title="We wunt be druv">
        We wunt be druv
       </a>
      </i>
      , is a
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_dialect" title="Sussex dialect">
       Sussex dialect
      </a>
      expression meaning 'we will not be pushed around' and reflects the traditionally independent nature of Sussex men and women. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Round-headed_Rampion" title="Round-headed Rampion">
       round-headed rampion
      </a>
      , also known as the 'Pride of Sussex', was adopted as Sussex's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County_flower" title="County flower">
       county flower
      </a>
      in 2002.
     </p>

      <span class="mw-headline" id="Landscape">
       Landscape
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Landscape">
        edit

      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages,_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_(looking_east_-_2003-05-26).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages%2C_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_%28looking_east_-_2003-05-26%29.jpg/220px-Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages%2C_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_%28looking_east_-_2003-05-26%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages%2C_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_%28looking_east_-_2003-05-26%29.jpg/330px-Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages%2C_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_%28looking_east_-_2003-05-26%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages%2C_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_%28looking_east_-_2003-05-26%29.jpg/440px-Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages%2C_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_%28looking_east_-_2003-05-26%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seven_Sisters_cliffs_and_the_coastguard_cottages,_from_Seaford_Head_showing_Cuckmere_Haven_(looking_east_-_2003-05-26).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The South Downs meets the sea at the
        <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters,_Sussex" title="Seven Sisters, Sussex">
         Seven Sisters
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The physical geography of Sussex relies heavily on its lying on the southern part of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weald%E2%80%93Artois_anticline" title="Weald–Artois anticline">
       Wealden anticline
      </a>
      , the major features of which are the high lands that cross the county in a west to east direction: the
      <a href="/wiki/Weald" title="Weald">
       Weald
      </a>
      itself and the
      <a href="/wiki/South_Downs" title="South Downs">
       South Downs
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_England" title="Natural England">
       Natural England
      </a>
      has identified the following seven
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_character_area" title="National character area">
       national character areas
      </a>
      in Sussex:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NE-NCAs_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NE-NCAs-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/South_Coast_Plain" title="South Coast Plain">
        South Coast Plain
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/South_Downs" title="South Downs">
        South Downs
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Greensand_Ridge" title="Greensand Ridge">
        Wealden Greensand
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Weald" title="Low Weald">
        Low Weald
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Weald" title="High Weald">
        High Weald
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Pevensey_Levels" title="Pevensey Levels">
        Pevensey Levels
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Romney_Marsh" title="Romney Marsh">
        Romney Marshes
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      At 280m,
      <a href="/wiki/Blackdown,_West_Sussex" title="Blackdown, West Sussex">
       Blackdown
      </a>
      is the highest point in Sussex, or
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_counties_by_highest_point" title="List of English counties by highest point">
       county top
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Ditchling_Beacon" title="Ditchling Beacon">
       Ditchling Beacon
      </a>
      (248m) is the highest point in East Sussex. At 113 kilometres (70 miles) long, the River Medway is the longest river flowing through Sussex. The longest river entirely in Sussex is the River Arun, which is 60 kilometres (37 miles) long. Sussex's largest lakes are man-made reservoirs. The largest is Bewl Water on the Kent border, while the largest wholly within Sussex is Ardingly Reservoir.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
       Climate
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Climate">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The coastal resorts of Sussex and neighbouring
      <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">
       Hampshire
      </a>
      are the sunniest places in the United Kingdom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetOff_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MetOff-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The coast has consistently more sunshine than the inland areas: sea breezes, blowing off the sea, tend to clear any cloud from the coast.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisSx_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-VisSx-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of Sussex lies in
      <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">
       Hardiness zone
      </a>
      8; the exception is the coastal plain west of Brighton, which lies in the milder zone 9.
     </p>
     <p>
      Rainfall is below average with the heaviest precipitation on the South Downs with 950 mm (37 in) of rainfall per year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisSx_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-VisSx-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The close proximity of Sussex to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Continent" title="The Continent">
       the Continent
      </a>
      of Europe, results in cold spells in winter and hot, humid weather in summer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisSx_13-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-VisSx-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The climate of the coastal districts is strongly influenced by the sea, which, because of its tendency to warm up slower than land, can result in cooler temperatures than inland in the summer. In the autumn months, the coast sometimes has higher temperatures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisSx_13-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-VisSx-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rainfall during the summer months is mainly from thunderstorms and thundery showers; from January to March the heavier rainfall is due to prevailing south-westerly frontal systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisSx_13-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-VisSx-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In winter, the east winds can be as cold as further inland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisSx_13-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-VisSx-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Selsey" title="Selsey">
       Selsey
      </a>
      is known as a
      <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">
       tornado
      </a>
      hotspot, with small tornadoes hitting the town in 1986, 1998 and 2000,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetOff_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MetOff-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with the 1998 tornado causing an estimated £10 million of damage to 1,000 buildings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetOff_12-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-MetOff-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The sunshine average is approximately 1900 hours a year, this is much higher than the UK average of 1340 hours a year.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conurbations">
       Conurbations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Conurbations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Most of Sussex's population is distributed in an east-west line along the English Channel coast or on the east-west line of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A272" title="A272">
       A272
      </a>
      . The exception to this pattern is the 20th century north-south development on the
      <a href="/wiki/A23_road" title="A23 road">
       A23
      </a>
      -
      <a href="/wiki/Brighton_Main_Line" title="Brighton Main Line">
       Brighton line
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Transport_corridor" title="Transport corridor">
       corridor
      </a>
      , Sussex's main link to London. Sussex's population is dominated by the Brighton/Worthing/Littlehampton conurbation that, with a population of over 470,000, is home to almost 1 in 3 of Sussex's population. According to the
      <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">
       ONS
      </a>
      urban area populations for continuous built-up areas, these are the 5 largest conurbations (population figures from the 2001 census):
     </p>
