     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Population">
       Population
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Population">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The combined population of Sussex is about 1.6 million.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ONS2011_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ONS2011-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [nb 1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2011, Sussex had a population density of 425 per km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      , higher than the average for England of 407 per km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest statement as to the population of Sussex is made by Bede, who describes the county as containing in 681 land of 7,000 families; allowing ten to a family (a reasonable estimate at that date), the total population would be 70,000.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1693 the county is stated to have contained 21,537 houses. The 1801 census found that the population was 159,311. The decline of the Sussex ironworks probably accounts for the small increase of population during several centuries, although after the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massacre_of_St_Bartholomew" title="Massacre of St Bartholomew">
       massacre of St Bartholomew
      </a>
      upwards of 1,500
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">
       Huguenots
      </a>
      landed at Rye, and in 1685, after the revocation of the
      <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Nantes" title="Edict of Nantes">
       Edict of Nantes
      </a>
      , many more refugees were added to the county.
     </p>
     <p>
      The area of the ancient county is 933,887 acres (377,931 ha) with a population in 1891 of 550,446 and in 1901 of 605,202.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sussex" title="History of Sussex">
       History of Sussex
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings">
       Beginnings
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Beginnings">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_heidelbergensis_(10233446).jpg">
        <img alt="Reconstruction of Homo heidelbergensis" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="529" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg/200px-Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg/300px-Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg/400px-Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_heidelbergensis_(10233446).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Reconstruction of
        <i>
         Homo heidelbergensis
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Finds at
      <a href="/wiki/Eartham_Pit,_Boxgrove" title="Eartham Pit, Boxgrove">
       Eartham Pit
      </a>
      in Boxgrove show that the area has some of the earliest hominid remains in Europe, dating back some 500,000 years and known as Boxgrove Man or
      <a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">
       Homo heidelbergensis
      </a>
      . At a site near Pulborough called The Beedings, tools have been found that date from around 35,000 years ago and that are thought to be from either the last
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">
       Neanderthals
      </a>
      in northern Europe or pioneer populations of modern humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The thriving population lived by hunting game such as horses, bison,
      <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">
       mammoth
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Woolly_rhinoceros" title="Woolly rhinoceros">
       woolly rhinos
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Around 6000BC the ice sheet over the North Sea melted, sea levels rose and the meltwaters burst south and westwards, creating the English Channel and cutting the people of Sussex off from their
      <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">
       Mesolithic
      </a>
      kinsmen to the south. Later in the
      <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">
       Neolithic
      </a>
      period, the area of the South Downs above Worthing was one of Britain's largest and most important
      <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">
       flint
      </a>
      -mining centres.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SSST10_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SSST10-21">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The flints were used to help fell trees for agriculture. The oldest of these mines, at Church Hill in Findon, has been carbon-dated to 4500BC to 3750BC, making it one of the earliest known mines in Britain. Flint tools from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cissbury" title="Cissbury">
       Cissbury
      </a>
      have been found as far away as the eastern Mediterranean.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SteyningMus_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SteyningMus-22">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sussex is rich in remains from the
      <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
       Bronze
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
       Iron Ages
      </a>
      , in particular the Bronze Age barrows known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Devil%27s_Jumps,_Treyford" title="Devil's Jumps, Treyford">
       Devil's Jumps
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cissbury_Ring" title="Cissbury Ring">
       Cissbury Ring
      </a>
      , one of Britain's largest hillforts. Towards the end of the Iron Age in 75BC people from the
      <a href="/wiki/Atrebates" title="Atrebates">
       Atrebates
      </a>
      , one of the tribes of the
      <a href="/wiki/Belgae" title="Belgae">
       Belgae
      </a>
      , a mix of Celtic and German stock, started invading and occupying southern Britain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armstrong24_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-armstrong24-23">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was followed by an invasion by the Roman army under
      <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">
       Julius Caesar
      </a>
      that temporarily occupied the south-east in 55BC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armstrong24_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-armstrong24-23">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soon after the first Roman invasion had ended, the Celtic
      <a href="/wiki/Regnenses" title="Regnenses">
       Regnenses
      </a>
      tribe under their leader
      <a href="/wiki/Commius" title="Commius">
       Commius
      </a>
      occupied the
      <a href="/wiki/Manhood_Peninsula" title="Manhood Peninsula">
       Manhood Peninsula
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armstrong24_23-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-armstrong24-23">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Tincomarus" title="Tincomarus">
       Tincomarus
      </a>
      and then
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cogidubnus" title="Cogidubnus">
       Cogidubnus
      </a>
      followed Commius as rulers of the Regnenses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armstrong24_23-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-armstrong24-23">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Canton">
       Roman Canton
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Roman Canton">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fishbourne_model.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1524" data-file-width="2484" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Fishbourne_model.JPG/220px-Fishbourne_model.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Fishbourne_model.JPG/330px-Fishbourne_model.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Fishbourne_model.JPG/440px-Fishbourne_model.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fishbourne_model.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Museum model of how Fishbourne Roman Palace may have appeared
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      At the time of the Roman conquest in AD43, there was an
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Oppidum" title="Oppidum">
        oppidum
       </a>
      </i>
      in the southern part of their territory, probably in the Selsey region.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cunliffe169_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cunliffe169-24">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A number of archaeologists now think there is a strong possibility that the
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">
       Roman invasion of Britain
      </a>
      in AD43 started around Fishbourne and Chichester Harbour rather than the traditional landing place of
      <a href="/wiki/Richborough" title="Richborough">
       Richborough
      </a>
      in Kent. According to this theory, the Romans were called to restore the refugee
      <a href="/wiki/Verica" title="Verica">
       Verica
      </a>
      , king of the Atrebates, who had been driven out by the
      <a href="/wiki/Catuvellauni" title="Catuvellauni">
       Catuvellauni
      </a>
      , a tribe based around modern Hertfordshire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sussex was home to the magnificent
      <a href="/wiki/Fishbourne_Roman_Palace" title="Fishbourne Roman Palace">
       Roman Palace at Fishbourne
      </a>
      , by far the largest Roman residence known north of the Alps. Much of Sussex was a Roman canton of the
      <a href="/wiki/Regnenses" title="Regnenses">
       Regnenses
      </a>
      or Regni, with its capital at
      <a href="/wiki/Noviomagus_Reginorum" title="Noviomagus Reginorum">
       Noviomagus Reginorum
      </a>
      , modern-day Chichester. The Romans built villas, especially on the coastal plain and around Chichester, one of the best preserved being that at
      <a href="/wiki/Bignor" title="Bignor">
       Bignor
      </a>
      . Christianity first came to Sussex at this time, but faded away when the Romans left in the 5th century. The nationally important Patching hoard of Roman coins that was found in 1997 is the latest find of Roman coins found in
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">
       Britain
      </a>
      , probably deposited after 475 AD, well after the
      <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">
       Roman departure from Britain
      </a>
      around 410 AD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white351_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-white351-26">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Saxon_Kingdom">
       Saxon Kingdom
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Saxon Kingdom">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sussex" title="Kingdom of Sussex">
       Kingdom of Sussex
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The foundation legend of Sussex is provided by the
      <i>
       Anglo-Saxon Chronicle
      </i>
      , which states that in the year AD 477
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lle_of_Sussex" title="Ælle of Sussex">
       Ælle
      </a>
      landed with his three sons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-welchase9_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-welchase9-27">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asc477_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-asc477-28">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Having fought on the banks of the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mercredesburne" title="Battle of Mercredesburne">
       Mearcredesburna
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      it seems Aelle secured the area between the Ouse and Cuckmere in a treaty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-welchess24_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-welchess24-30">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After Aelle’s forces seized the
      <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Shore" title="Saxon Shore">
       Saxon Shore
      </a>
      fort of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anderida" title="Anderida">
       Anderida
      </a>
      , the South Saxons were able to gradually colonise free of Romano-British control and extend their territory westwards to link with the Saxon settlement at
      <a href="/wiki/Highdown_Hill" title="Highdown Hill">
       Highdown Hill
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-welchess24_30-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-welchess24-30">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Aelle was recognised as the first '
      <a href="/wiki/Bretwalda" title="Bretwalda">
       Bretwalda
      </a>
      ' or overlord of southern Britain. He was probably the most senior of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" title="Anglo-Saxon">
       Anglo-Saxon
      </a>
      kings and led the ill-fated campaign against
      <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">
       King Arthur
      </a>
      at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Badon" title="Mount Badon">
       Mount Badon
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swilfrid1.jpg">
        <img alt="An engraving, which is a 17th-century copy, of an earlier painted Tudor mural in Chichester cathedral depicting Cædwalla confirming the granting of land to Wilfrid to build his monastery in Selsey" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="381" data-file-width="658" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Swilfrid1.jpg/250px-Swilfrid1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Swilfrid1.jpg/375px-Swilfrid1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Swilfrid1.jpg/500px-Swilfrid1.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swilfrid1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Engraving showing Cædwalla confirming the granting of land to Wilfrid to build his monastery in Selsey.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      By the end of the 7th century, the region around Selsey and Chichester had become the political centre of the kingdom. In the 660s-670s,
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwealh_of_Sussex" title="Æthelwealh of Sussex">
       King Aethelwealh
      </a>
      of Sussex formed an alliance with the Mercian king
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wulfhere" title="Wulfhere">
       Wulfhere
      </a>
      and together they took the Isle of Wight from the West Saxons, probably at the battle of Biedanheafele. As Mercia's first Christian king, Wulfhere insisted that Æthelwealh also convert to Christianity. Æthelwealh was
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptised" title="Baptised">
       baptised
      </a>
      in Mercia, with Wulfhere as his
      <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">
       sponsor
      </a>
      . Wulfhere gave the Isle of Wight and Meon Valley to Aethelwealh, with Wulfhere acting as
      <a href="/wiki/Lord" title="Lord">
       overlord
      </a>
      . The alliance with Mercia was sealed with Æthelwealh taking the hand of Eabe, a Mercian princess in marriage.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid" title="Wilfrid">
       Wilfrid
      </a>
      , the exiled bishop of York, came to Sussex in 681 and with King Æthelwealh's approval set up a mission to convert the people of Sussex to
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      . Æthelwealh gave Wilfrid land on the Manhood peninsula, close to his own royal estate and Wilfrid founded
      <a href="/wiki/Selsey_Abbey" title="Selsey Abbey">
       Selsey Abbey
      </a>
      . The mission was jeopardised when King Æthelwealh was killed by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%A6dwalla" title="Cædwalla">
       Cædwalla
      </a>
      , a prince of Wessex. Cædwalla confirmed Æthelwealh's grant of land and Wilfrid built his Selsey Abbey. Cædwalla was driven out by the South Saxon nobles
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berthun" title="Berthun">
       Berthun
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andhun" title="Andhun">
       Andhun
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The South Saxons fought off the West Saxons in 722 and again in 725. At the end of the 8th century,
      <a href="/wiki/Ealdwulf_of_Sussex" title="Ealdwulf of Sussex">
       Ealdwulf
      </a>
      was perhaps the last independent king of Sussex, after which Sussex and other southern kingdoms came increasingly under Mercian rule. Mercia's grip was shattered in 825 at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ellendun" title="Battle of Ellendun">
       battle of Ellendun
      </a>
      , after which Sussex and the other southern kingdoms came under the control of Wessex, which later grew into the kingdom of England.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Norman_Sussex">
       Norman Sussex
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Norman Sussex">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harold_stone.JPG">
        <img alt="Picture of plaque at Battle Abbey, the traditional site of the High Altar of Battle Abbey founded to commemorate the victory of Duke William on 14 October 1066. The high altar was placed to mark the spot where King Harold died." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Harold_stone.JPG/250px-Harold_stone.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Harold_stone.JPG/375px-Harold_stone.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Harold_stone.JPG/500px-Harold_stone.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harold_stone.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Battle Abbey was founded to commemorate William's victory in the Battle of Hastings. The high altar was placed to mark the spot where King Harold died.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Sussex was the venue for the momentous
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">
       Battle of Hastings
      </a>
      , the decisive victory in the
      <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">
       Norman conquest of England
      </a>
      . In September 1066,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_of_Normandy" title="William of Normandy">
       William of Normandy
      </a>
      landed with his forces at
      <a href="/wiki/Pevensey" title="Pevensey">
       Pevensey
      </a>
      and erected a wooden castle at
      <a href="/wiki/Hastings" title="Hastings">
       Hastings
      </a>
      , from which they raided the surrounding area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bates79_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bates79-31">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The battle was fought between Duke William of Normandy and the English king,
      <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">
       Harold Godwinson
      </a>
      , who had strong connections with Sussex and whose chief
      <a href="/wiki/Family_seat" title="Family seat">
       seat
      </a>
      was probably in
      <a href="/wiki/Bosham" title="Bosham">
       Bosham
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After having marched his exhausted army all the way from Yorkshire, Harold fought the Normans at the Battle of Hastings, where England's army was defeated and Harold was killed. It is likely that all the fighting men of Sussex were at the battle, as the county's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thegns" title="Thegns">
       thegns
      </a>
      were decimated and any that survived had their lands confiscated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seward5_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seward5-34">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      William built
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_Abbey" title="Battle Abbey">
       Battle Abbey
      </a>
      at the site of the battle, with the exact spot where Harold fell marked by the high altar.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seward5_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-seward5-34">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sussex experienced some of the greatest changes of any English county under the Normans, for it was the heartland of King Harold and was potentially vulnerable to further invasion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxLand_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxLand-35">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The county was of great importance to the Normans; Hastings and Pevensey being on the most direct route for Normandy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armstrong48_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-armstrong48-36">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The county's existing sub-divisions, known as rapes, were made into castleries and each territory was given to one of William's most trusted barons. Castles were built to defend the territories including at Arundel, Bramber, Lewes, Pevensey and Hastings. Sussex's bishop,
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelric_II" title="Æthelric II">
       Æthelric II
      </a>
      , was deposed and imprisoned and replaced with and William the Conqueror's personal chaplain,
      <a href="/wiki/Stigand_of_Selsey" title="Stigand of Selsey">
       Stigand
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kelly9_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kelly9-37">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Normans also built
      <a href="/wiki/Chichester_Cathedral" title="Chichester Cathedral">
       Chichester Cathedral
      </a>
      and moved the seat of Sussex's bishopric from Selsey to Chichester. The Normans also founded new towns in Sussex, including New Shoreham (the centre of modern Shoreham-by-Sea), Battle, Arundel, Uckfield and Winchelsea.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxLand_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxLand-35">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1264, the Sussex Downs were the location of the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lewes" title="Battle of Lewes">
       Battle of Lewes
      </a>
      , in which
      <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort,_6th_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Simon de Montfort, 6th Earl of Leicester">
       Simon de Montfort
      </a>
      and his fellow barons captured Prince Edward (later
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">
       Edward I
      </a>
      ), the son and heir of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">
       Henry III
      </a>
      . The subsequent treaty, known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Mise_of_Lewes" title="Mise of Lewes">
       Mise of Lewes
      </a>
      , led to Montfort summoning the
      <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort%27s_Parliament" title="Simon de Montfort's Parliament">
       first parliament
      </a>
      in English history without any prior royal authorisation. A provisional administration was set up, consisting of Montfort, the
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Bersted" title="Stephen Bersted">
       Bishop of Chichester
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_de_Clare,_7th_Earl_of_Gloucester" title="Gilbert de Clare, 7th Earl of Gloucester">
       Earl of Gloucester
      </a>
      . These three were to elect a council of nine, to govern until a permanent settlement could be reached.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sussex_under_the_Plantagenets">
       Sussex under the Plantagenets
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sussex under the Plantagenets">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      During the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hundred_Years_War" title="Hundred Years War">
       Hundred Years War
      </a>
      , Sussex found itself on the frontline, convenient both for intended invasions and retaliatory expeditions by licensed French pirates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowerson_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lowerson-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hastings, Rye and Winchelsea were all burnt during this period
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowerson_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-lowerson-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and all three towns became part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Cinque_Ports" title="Cinque Ports">
       Cinque Ports
      </a>
      , a loose federation for supplying ships for the country's security. Also at this time,
      <a href="/wiki/Amberley_Castle" title="Amberley Castle">
       Amberley
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bodiam_Castle" title="Bodiam Castle">
       Bodiam
      </a>
      castles were built to defend the upper reaches of navigable rivers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowerson_9-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-lowerson-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_Sussex">
       Early modern Sussex
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Early modern Sussex">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Like the rest of the country, the Church of England's split with Rome during the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">
       Henry VIII
      </a>
      was felt in Sussex.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkinson52_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wilkinson52-39">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1538 there was a royal order for the demolition of the shrine of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Richard" title="Saint Richard">
       Saint Richard
      </a>
      , in Chichester Cathedral,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stephens213_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stephens213-40">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with Thomas Cromwell saying that there was "a certain kind of idolatry about the shrine".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stephens213_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-stephens213-40">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">
       Queen Mary
      </a>
      , 41 people in Sussex were burnt at the stake for their Protestant beliefs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkinson52_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-wilkinson52-39">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Elizabeth re-established the break with Rome when she passed the 1559
      <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy_1558" title="Act of Supremacy 1558">
       Acts of Supremacy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1558" title="Act of Uniformity 1558">
       Uniformity
      </a>
      . Under
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">
       Elizabeth I
      </a>
      , religious intolerance continued albeit on a lesser scale, with several people being executed for their Catholic beliefs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowerson_9-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-lowerson-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sussex escaped the worst ravages of the
      <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">
       English Civil War
      </a>
      , although in 1642 there were
      <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">
       sieges
      </a>
      at Arundel and Chichester, and a skirmish at Haywards Heath when
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royalists" title="Royalists">
       Royalists
      </a>
      marching towards Lewes were intercepted by local
      <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">
       Parliamentarians
      </a>
      . The Royalists were routed with around 200 killed or taken prisoner.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CivWar_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CivWar-41">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite its being under Parliamentarian control,
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">
       Charles II
      </a>
      was able to
      <a href="/wiki/Escape_of_Charles_II" title="Escape of Charles II">
       journey through the county
      </a>
      after the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">
       Battle of Worcester
      </a>
      in 1651 to make his escape to France from the port of Shoreham.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern_and_contemporary_Sussex">
       Late modern and contemporary Sussex
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Late modern and contemporary Sussex">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Sussex coast was greatly modified by the social movement of
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_bathing" title="Sea bathing">
       sea bathing
      </a>
      for health which became fashionable among the wealthy in the second half of the 18th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxLand_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxLand-35">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Resorts developed all along the coast, including at Brighton, Hastings, Worthing, and Bognor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxLand_35-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxLand-35">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the beginning of the 19th century agricultural labourers' conditions took a turn for the worse with an increasing amount of them becoming unemployed, those in work faced their wages being forced down.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrison249_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-harrison249-42">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Conditions became so bad that it was even reported to the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">
       House of Lords
      </a>
      in 1830 that four harvest labourers (seasonal workers) had been found dead of starvation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrison249_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-harrison249-42">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The deteriorating conditions of work for the agricultural labourer eventually triggered riots, first in neighbouring Kent, and then in Sussex, where they lasted for several weeks, although the unrest continued until 1832 and became known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Swing_Riots" title="Swing Riots">
       Swing Riots
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrison249_42-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-harrison249-42">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horspool339_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-horspool339-43">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Railways spread across Sussex in the 19th century and county councils were created for Sussex's eastern and western divisions in 1889.
     </p>
     <p>
      During
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      , on the eve of the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">
       Battle of the Somme
      </a>
      on 30 June 1916, the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Sussex_Regiment" title="Royal Sussex Regiment">
       Royal Sussex Regiment
      </a>
      took part in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Boar%27s_Head" title="Battle of the Boar's Head">
       Battle of the Boar's Head
      </a>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/Richebourg-l%27Avou%C3%A9" title="Richebourg-l'Avoué">
       Richebourg-l'Avoué
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richebourg_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Richebourg-44">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The day subsequently became known as
      <b>
       The Day Sussex Died
      </b>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richebourg_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Richebourg-44">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Over a period of less than five hours the 17 officers and 349 men were killed, including 12 sets of brothers, including three from one family.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richebourg_44-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Richebourg-44">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A further 1,000 men were wounded or taken prisoner.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richebourg_44-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Richebourg-44">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With the declaration of the
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      , Sussex found itself part of the country's frontline with its airfields playing a key role in the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">
       Battle of Britain
      </a>
      and with its towns being some of the most frequently bombed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leslie118_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-leslie118-45">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the Sussex regiments served overseas, the defence of the county was undertaken by units of the
      <a href="/wiki/Home_Guard_(United_Kingdom)" title="Home Guard (United Kingdom)">
       Home Guard
      </a>
      with help from the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Canadian_Army" title="First Canadian Army">
       First Canadian Army
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leslie118_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-leslie118-45">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brandon302_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-brandon302-46">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the lead up to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-Day" title="D-Day">
       D-Day
      </a>
      landings, the people of Sussex were witness to the buildup of military personnel and materials, including the assembly of landing crafts and construction of
      <a href="/wiki/Mulberry_harbour" title="Mulberry harbour">
       Mulberry harbours
      </a>
      off the county's coast.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brandon302_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-brandon302-46">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the post-war era, the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Towns_Act_1946" title="New Towns Act 1946">
       New Towns Act 1946
      </a>
      designated Crawley as the site of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_town" title="New town">
       new town
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuppMem_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SuppMem-47">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1972" title="Local Government Act 1972">
       Local Government Act 1972
      </a>
      , the eastern and western divisions of Sussex were made into the ceremonial counties of East and West Sussex in 1974. Boundaries were changed and a large part of the rape of Lewes was transferred from the eastern division into West Sussex, along with Gatwick Airport, which was historically part of the county of Surrey.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">
       Governance
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Governance">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_local_government_in_Sussex" title="History of local government in Sussex">
       History of local government in Sussex
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">
       Politics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Politics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sussex (UK Parliament constituency)">
       Sussex (UK Parliament constituency)
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/High_Sheriff_of_Sussex" title="High Sheriff of Sussex">
       High Sheriff of Sussex
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Sussex" title="Lord Lieutenant of Sussex">
       Lord Lieutenant of Sussex
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Custos_Rotulorum_of_Sussex" title="Custos Rotulorum of Sussex">
       Custos Rotulorum of Sussex
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      From 1290, Sussex returned two
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Members_of_Parliament" title="Members of Parliament">
       Members of Parliament
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">
       House of Commons
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">
       Parliament of England
      </a>
      . Each county returned two MPs and each borough designated by Royal charter also returned two MPs. After the
      <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">
       union
      </a>
      with Scotland two members represented the county in the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_Great_Britain" title="House of Commons of Great Britain">
       House of Commons of Great Britain
      </a>
      from 1707 to 1800 and of the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">
       House of Commons of the United Kingdom
      </a>
      from 1801 to 1832. After the Reform Act 1832 some of the larger industrial towns of northern England were enfranchised for the first time and smaller English boroughs (known as Rotten Boroughs) were disenfranchised, including Bramber, East Grinstead, Seaford, Steyning and Winchelsea in Sussex.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leslie72_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-leslie72-48">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Reform Act of 1832 divided what had been a single county constituency into
      <a href="/wiki/East_Sussex_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Sussex (UK Parliament constituency)">
       eastern
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/West_Sussex_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="West Sussex (UK Parliament constituency)">
       western
      </a>
      divisions, with two representatives elected for each division.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horsefieldapp23_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-horsefieldapp23-49">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The reforms of the 19th century made the electoral system more representative, but it was not until 1928 that there was universal suffrage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leslie72_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-leslie72-48">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Law">
       Law
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Law">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Headquartered in Lewes,
      <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">
       Home Office
      </a>
      policing in Sussex has been provided by
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_Police" title="Sussex Police">
       Sussex Police
      </a>
      since 1968.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxPolRec_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxPolRec-50">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg/150px-Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg/225px-Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg/300px-Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lewes_Law_Courts1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Lewes Crown Court is the first-tier Crown Court for Sussex
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first-tier
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_Court" title="Crown Court">
       Crown Court
      </a>
      for all of Sussex is
      <a href="/wiki/Lewes_Crown_Court" title="Lewes Crown Court">
       Lewes Crown Court
      </a>
      , which has courts in Lewes, Brighton and Hove. Like other first-tier Crown Courts it has its own resident
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Court_Judge" title="High Court Judge">
       High Court Judge
      </a>
      . There is also a third-tier Crown Court at Chichester. The
      <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_security_categories_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prisoner security categories in the United Kingdom">
       local
      </a>
      prison in Sussex for men is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewes_(HM_Prison)" title="Lewes (HM Prison)">
       Lewes Prison
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Old_Pol_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Old_Pol-51">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and there is also a Category D prison at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford_(HM_Prison)" title="Ford (HM Prison)">
       Ford
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">
       Administrative divisions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_sub-divisions">
       Historic sub-divisions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Historic sub-divisions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Rape_(county_subdivision)" title="Rape (county subdivision)">
       Rape (county subdivision)
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1073" data-file-width="2500" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png/220px-Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png/330px-Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png/440px-Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sussex_administrative_map_1832.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Map of Sussex in 1851 showing the six Rapes
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A rape was a traditional sub-division of the county of Sussex. Their origin is unknown, but they appear to predate the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">
       Norman Conquest
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Each rape was split into several
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hundred_(division)" title="Hundred (division)">
       hundreds
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      At the time of the Norman Conquest, there were four rapes: Arundel, Lewes, Pevensey and Hastings. The
      <a href="/wiki/Rape_of_Bramber" title="Rape of Bramber">
       rape of Bramber
      </a>
      was created later in the 11th century and the
      <a href="/wiki/Rape_of_Chichester" title="Rape of Chichester">
       rape of Chichester
      </a>
      was created in the 13th century.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_local_authority_areas">
       Modern local authority areas
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Modern local authority areas">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Sussex is divided into two administrative counties and one unitary authority, Brighton and Hove. The divisions of east Sussex and west Sussex have their roots in the church's division of the county at the river Adur into east and west parts (divided from at least the 11th century into the
      <a href="/wiki/Archdeacon_of_Chichester" title="Archdeacon of Chichester">
       archdeaconry of Chichester
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archdeaconry_of_Lewes" title="Archdeaconry of Lewes">
       archdeaconry of Lewes
      </a>
      ). With Sussex's cathedral located at Chichester in the far west of the county, 9 miles from the western boundary and 90 miles from the eastern boundary, it became practical to divide the county into two sections. The three eastern rapes of Sussex became east Sussex and the three western rapes became west Sussex.
     </p>
     <p>
      An act of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">
       Henry VII
      </a>
      dating from 1504 directed that, for convenience, the two halves of the county should have separate administrations (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarter_Sessions" title="Quarter Sessions">
       Quarter Sessions
      </a>
      ). This situation was recognised by the County of Sussex Act 1865. Under the
      <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1888" title="Local Government Act 1888">
       Local Government Act 1888
      </a>
      , the two divisions became two
      <a href="/wiki/Administrative_counties_of_England" title="Administrative counties of England">
       administrative counties
      </a>
      (along with three
      <a href="/wiki/County_borough" title="County borough">
       county boroughs
      </a>
      : Brighton,
      <a href="/wiki/Hastings" title="Hastings">
       Hastings
      </a>
      and, from 1911,
      <a href="/wiki/Eastbourne" title="Eastbourne">
       Eastbourne
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Monarchy_and_peerage">
       Monarchy and peerage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Monarchy and peerage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kings_of_Sussex" title="Kings of Sussex">
       Kings of Sussex
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Sussex" title="Duke of Sussex">
       Duke of Sussex
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Sussex" title="Earl of Sussex">
       Earl of Sussex
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Created in the fifth century, the kingdom of Sussex continued until its absorption into the kingdom of Wessex and later England in 825. In charters, Sussex's monarchs were sometimes referred to as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ealdormen" title="Ealdormen">
       ealdormen
      </a>
      , or Dux in Latin, which is sometimes translated as 'duke'. The early Earls of Arundel, up to 1243, were often also called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earls_of_Sussex" title="Earls of Sussex">
       Earls of Sussex
      </a>
      . In 1801 the title of
      <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Sussex" title="Duke of Sussex">
       Duke of Sussex
      </a>
      was created and conferred on
      <a href="/wiki/Prince_Augustus_Frederick,_Duke_of_Sussex" title="Prince Augustus Frederick, Duke of Sussex">
       The Prince Augustus Frederick
      </a>
      , the sixth son of
      <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">
       George III
      </a>
      . Since he had no legitimate issue, the title became extinct on his death in 1843.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WebEngHist_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WebEngHist-54">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although there was speculation that the dukedom would pass to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_Andrew" title="Prince Andrew">
       Prince Andrew
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_William" title="Prince William">
       Prince William
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mirrorduke_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mirrorduke-55">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the title is currently one of six inactive British royal titles.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
       Economy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Economy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Much of the Sussex Weald consists of wet sticky clays or drought-prone acid sands and is often broken up into to small irregular fields and woods by the topography, making it unsuitable for intensive arable farming. In these areas pastoral or mixed farming has always been the pattern, with field boundaries often little changed since the medieval period. Sussex cattle are the descendants of the draught oxen, which continued to be used in the Weald longer than in other parts of England. The chalk downlands were traditionally grazed by large numbers of small
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southdown_(sheep)" title="Southdown (sheep)">
       Southdown
      </a>
      sheep, suited to the low fertility of the pasture, until the coming of artificial fertiliser made cereal growing worthwhile. There are still fishing fleets, notably at Rye and Hastings, but the number of boats is much reduced. There are working harbours at Rye, Hastings, Newhaven and Shoreham; whilst Pagham and Chichester harbours cater for leisure craft, as does Brighton Marina. Sussex had an iron working industry since the Roman period. The large supply of wood in the county made it a favourable centre for the industry, all smelting being done with charcoal till the middle of the 18th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ankers46_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ankers46-56">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The glass making industry started on the Sussex/Surrey border in the early 13th century and flourished till the 17th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brandon175_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-brandon175-57">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Mid Sussex area had a thriving clay industry in the first quarter of the 20th century.
     </p>
     <p>
      Sussex has considerable variation in wealth and deprivation.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_Value_Added" title="Gross Value Added">
       Gross Value Added
      </a>
      , a measure of
      <a href="/wiki/Output_(economics)" title="Output (economics)">
       output
      </a>
      , was £27,464 million for Sussex in 2009.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ONS-NUTS_GVA_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ONS-NUTS_GVA-58">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [nb 2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      GVA per head in 2009 for Sussex was £17,590, higher than neighbouring Kent but around 86 per cent of the UK average of £20,341.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ONS-NUTS_GVAperhead_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ONS-NUTS_GVAperhead-60">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [nb 3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      GVA per capita varies significantly between Sussex's three upper tier local authority areas: in 2009 Brighton and Hove had the highest GVA per head in Sussex with an average of £20,611 (101 per cent of the UK average).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ONS-NUTS_GVAperhead_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ONS-NUTS_GVAperhead-60">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The local authority area of East Sussex has the lowest with a GVA per head of £13,521, 66 per cent of the UK average.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ONS-NUTS_GVAperhead_60-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ONS-NUTS_GVAperhead-60">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is also serious deprivation in Sussex comparable to the most deprived UK inner city areas. Some areas of Sussex are in the top 5 per cent most deprived in the UK and, in some areas, two-thirds of children are living in poverty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxUnc_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxUnc-62">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2011, two
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_Enterprise_Partnership" title="Local Enterprise Partnership">
       Local Enterprise Partnerships
      </a>
      were formed to improve the economy in Sussex. These were the Coast to Capital LEP, covering West Sussex, Brighton and Hove and the Lewes district in the west of East Sussex, as well as parts of Surrey and South London; and the South East LEP, which covers the local authority area of East Sussex, as well as Kent and Essex. In the most populous part of Sussex, around the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brighton/Worthing/Littlehampton" title="Brighton/Worthing/Littlehampton">
       Brighton and Hove Built-up area
      </a>
      , the Greater Brighton City Deal was formed to enable the area to fulfil its economic potential, into one of the highest performing urban economies in the UK.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBCD_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GBCD-63">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tourism in Sussex is well-established, and includes seaside resorts and the
      <a href="/wiki/South_Downs_National_Park" title="South Downs National Park">
       South Downs National Park
      </a>
      . Brighton and Hove has a high density of businesses involved in media, particularly digital or "
      <a href="/wiki/New_media" title="New media">
       new media
      </a>
      "; since the 1990s Brighton has been referred to as "Silicon Beach".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Greater Brighton City Deal seeks to develop Brighton’s creative-tech cluster under the name Tech City South.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBCD_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GBCD-63">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sussex" title="University of Sussex">
       University of Sussex
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Brighton" title="University of Brighton">
       University of Brighton
      </a>
      provide employment for many more. A large part of the county, centred on
      <a href="/wiki/Gatwick_Airport" title="Gatwick Airport">
       Gatwick Airport
      </a>
      has been recognised as a key economic growth area for South East England
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GwkDi_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GwkDi-65">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      whilst reasonable rail connections allow many people to work in
      <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">
       London
      </a>
      . Several large companies are based in Sussex including
      <a href="/wiki/American_Express" title="American Express">
       American Express
      </a>
      (Brighton),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/The_Body_Shop" title="The Body Shop">
       The Body Shop
      </a>
      (Littlehampton),
      <a href="/wiki/Bowers_%26_Wilkins" title="Bowers &amp; Wilkins">
       Bowers &amp; Wilkins
      </a>
      (Worthing),
      <a href="/wiki/Hastings_Insurance" title="Hastings Insurance">
       Hastings Insurance
      </a>
      (Bexhill),
      <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_plc" title="Ricardo plc">
       Ricardo plc
      </a>
      (Shoreham-by-Sea),
      <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Motor_Cars" title="Rolls-Royce Motor Cars">
       Rolls-Royce Motor Cars
      </a>
      (Goodwood),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales_UK" title="Thales UK">
       Thales UK
      </a>
      (Crawley),
      <a href="/wiki/Alfa_Laval" title="Alfa Laval">
       Alfa Laval
      </a>
      (Eastbourne) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Atlantic_Airways" title="Virgin Atlantic Airways">
       Virgin Atlantic Airways
      </a>
      (Crawley).
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
       Education
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Education">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg/150px-Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg/225px-Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg/300px-Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airborne_imagery_University_Of_Sussex_Campus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Aerial view of the University of Sussex
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The earliest university in Sussex is the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sussex" title="University of Sussex">
       University of Sussex
      </a>
      , founded in 1961
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USx_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-USx-67">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      at Falmer in Brighton, the first new university in England since World War Two. It has since been joined by the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Brighton" title="University of Brighton">
       University of Brighton
      </a>
      in 1992 and the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chichester" title="University of Chichester">
       University of Chichester
      </a>
      in 2005.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UChi_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UChi-68">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higher_Education" title="Higher Education">
       Higher Education
      </a>
      is also provided at
      <a href="/wiki/University_Centre_Hastings" title="University Centre Hastings">
       University Centre Hastings
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Northbrook_College" title="Northbrook College">
       Northbrook College
      </a>
      , whose West Durrington campus is referred to as University Centre Worthing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCW_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UCW-69">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/The_Prebendal_School" title="The Prebendal School">
       The Prebendal School
      </a>
      in Chichester is the oldest known school in Sussex
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preb_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Preb-70">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and probably dates to when the Normans moved the Sussex bishopric from
      <a href="/wiki/Selsey_Abbey" title="Selsey Abbey">
       Selsey
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Chichester_Cathedral" title="Chichester Cathedral">
       Chichester Cathedral
      </a>
      in the 11th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preb_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Preb-70">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Primary and secondary education in the state sector in Sussex is provided by its three
      <a href="/wiki/Local_education_authority" title="Local education authority">
       local education authorities
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/East_Sussex_County_Council" title="East Sussex County Council">
       East
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/West_Sussex_County_Council" title="West Sussex County Council">
       West Sussex County Councils
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Brighton_and_Hove_City_Council" title="Brighton and Hove City Council">
       Brighton and Hove City Council
      </a>
      . Sussex also has some of the best-known
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_schools" title="Independent schools">
       independent schools
      </a>
      in England including
      <a href="/wiki/Brighton_College" title="Brighton College">
       Brighton College
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lancing_College" title="Lancing College">
       Lancing College
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Roedean_School" title="Roedean School">
       Roedean School
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare">
       Healthcare
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Healthcare">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1086" data-file-width="1514" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png/150px-Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png/225px-Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3f/Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png/300px-Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital2.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The main building of the Royal Sussex County Hospital
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Sussex County Hospital (now the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Sussex_County_Hospital" title="Royal Sussex County Hospital">
       Royal Sussex County Hospital
      </a>
      ) was founded in 1828 at Brighton
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSCH-History_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RSCH-History-71">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      whilst the Sussex County Mental Asylum (later 'St. Francis Hospital' and now the
      <a href="/wiki/Princess_Royal_Hospital_(Haywards_Heath)" title="Princess Royal Hospital (Haywards Heath)">
       Princess Royal Hospital
      </a>
      ) was founded in 1859 in the centre of county at Haywards Heath.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheTimeChamber-SxCA_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TheTimeChamber-SxCA-72">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sussex's first
      <a href="/wiki/Medical_school" title="Medical school">
       medical school
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Brighton_and_Sussex_Medical_School" title="Brighton and Sussex Medical School">
       Brighton and Sussex Medical School
      </a>
      , was set up in 2002. In 2011 the four Sussex NHS
      <a href="/wiki/NHS_primary_care_trust" title="NHS primary care trust">
       primary care trusts
      </a>
      (PCTs) joined forces to become NHS Sussex.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHSSx_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NHSSx-73">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Major Trauma Centre at the Royal Sussex County Hospital is the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trauma_centre" title="Trauma centre">
       Major Trauma Centre
      </a>
      for Sussex with the Sussex's other hospitals acting as trauma units. It is one of only five major trauma centres across the NHS's South of England area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-TC_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBC-TC-74">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The hospital also houses the Sussex Cancer Centre which serves most of Sussex.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sx_Cancer_Centre_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sx_Cancer_Centre-75">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxCancerNetwork_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxCancerNetwork-76">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">
       Culture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Culture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Sussex" title="Culture of Sussex">
       Culture of Sussex
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Sussex has a centuries-old reputation for being separate and culturally distinct from the rest of England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxPeople_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxPeople-77">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The people of Sussex have a reputation for independence of thought
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESx_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ESx-78">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and have an aversion to being pushed around, as expressed through the Sussex motto,
      <a href="/wiki/We_wunt_be_druv" title="We wunt be druv">
       We wunt be druv
      </a>
      . Sussex is known for its strong tradition of
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_Bonfire_Societies" title="Sussex Bonfire Societies">
       bonfire celebrations
      </a>
      and its proud musical heritage. The county is home to England's largest arts festival, the
      <a href="/wiki/Brighton_Festival" title="Brighton Festival">
       Brighton Festival
      </a>
      . Chichester is home to the
      <a href="/wiki/Chichester_Festival_Theatre" title="Chichester Festival Theatre">
       Chichester Festival Theatre
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Pallant_House_Gallery" title="Pallant House Gallery">
       Pallant House Gallery
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">
       Architecture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Architecture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Sussex's building materials reflect its geology, being made of
      <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">
       flint
      </a>
      on and near the South Downs and
      <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">
       sandstone
      </a>
      in the Weald.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NandP17_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NandP17-79">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Brick" title="Brick">
       Brick
      </a>
      is used across the county.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NandP17_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NandP17-79">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg/150px-Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg/225px-Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg/300px-Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brighton_Royal_Pavilion.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Royal Pavilion, Brighton
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Typically conservative and moderate,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NandP56_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NandP56-80">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the architecture of Sussex also has elaborate and eccentric buildings rarely matched elsewhere in England including the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saxon_architecture" title="Saxon architecture">
       Saxon
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St_Mary_the_Blessed_Virgin,_Sompting" title="Church of St Mary the Blessed Virgin, Sompting">
       Church of St Mary the Blessed Virgin, Sompting
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Castle_Goring" title="Castle Goring">
       Castle Goring
      </a>
      , which has a front and rear of entirely different styles and Brighton's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Saracenic" title="Indo-Saracenic">
       Indo-Saracenic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Pavilion" title="Royal Pavilion">
       Royal Pavilion
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialect">
       Dialect
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Dialect">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Sussex_dialect" title="Sussex dialect">
       Sussex dialect
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Historically, Sussex has had its own dialect with regional differences reflecting its cultural history. It has been divided into variants for the three western rapes of West Sussex, the two eastern rapes of Lewes and Pevensey and an area approximate to the easternmost rape of Hastings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxPeople_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxPeople-77">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sussex dialect is also notable in having an unusually large number of words for mud, in a way similar to the popular belief which exists that the Inuit have an
      <a href="/wiki/Eskimo_words_for_snow" title="Eskimo words for snow">
       unusually large number of words for snow
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SxMisc_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SxMisc-82">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
       Literature
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sussex&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Literature">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Writers born in Sussex include the
      <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
       Renaissance
      </a>
      poet
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_May" title="Thomas May">
       Thomas May
      </a>
      and playwights Thomas Otway, and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Fletcher_(playwright)" title="John Fletcher (playwright)">
       John Fletcher
      </a>
      . One of the most prolific playwrights of his day, Fletcher is thought to have collaborated with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">
       Shakespeare
      </a>
      . Notable Sussex poets include
      <a href="/wiki/William_Hayley" title="William Hayley">
       William Hayley
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/William_Collins_(poet)" title="William Collins (poet)">
       William Collins
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">
       Percy Bysshe Shelley
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-PBS_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBC-PBS-83">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Wilfrid_Scawen_Blunt" title="Wilfrid Scawen Blunt">
       Wilfrid Scawen Blunt
      </a>
